</div>
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Planetary boundaries</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Planetary_boundary_layer" title="Planetary boundary layer">Planetary boundary layer</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Planetary_Boundaries_2015.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Planetary_Boundaries_2015.svg/400px-Planetary_Boundaries_2015.svg.png" width="400" height="400" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Planetary_Boundaries_2015.svg/600px-Planetary_Boundaries_2015.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Planetary_Boundaries_2015.svg/800px-Planetary_Boundaries_2015.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Planetary_Boundaries_2015.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Planetary boundaries according to Rockström <i>et al</i>. 2009<sup id="cite_ref-Rockstr.C3.B6m_2009_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rockstr.C3.B6m_2009-1">[1]</a></sup> and Steffen <i>et al</i>. 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The green areas represent human activities that are within safe margins, the yellow areas represent human activities that may or may not have exceeded safe margins, the red areas represent human activities that have exceeded safe margins, and the gray areas with red question marks represent human activities for which safe margins have not yet been determined.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Planetary boundaries</b> is the central concept in an Earth system framework proposed by a group of <a href="/wiki/Earth_system_science" title="Earth system science">Earth system</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_science" title="Environmental science">environmental scientists</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Johan_Rockstr%C3%B6m" title="Johan Rockström">Johan Rockström</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_Resilience_Centre" title="Stockholm Resilience Centre">Stockholm Resilience Centre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Will_Steffen" title="Will Steffen">Will Steffen</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Australian_National_University" title="Australian National University">Australian National University</a>. In 2009, the group proposed a framework of “planetary boundaries” designed to define a “safe operating space for humanity” for the international community, including governments at all levels, international organizations, civil society, the scientific community and the <a href="/wiki/Private_sector" title="Private sector">private sector</a>, as a precondition for <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">sustainable development</a>. This framework is based on scientific research that indicates that since the Industrial Revolution, human actions have gradually become the main driver of global environmental change. The scientists assert that once human activity has passed certain thresholds or tipping points, defined as “planetary boundaries”, there is a risk of “irreversible and abrupt environmental change”.<sup id="cite_ref-GSPReport2012_01_30_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GSPReport2012_01_30-3">[3]</a></sup> The scientists identified nine Earth system processes which have boundaries that, to the extent that they are not crossed, mark the safe zone for the planet. However, because of human activities some of these dangerous boundaries have already been crossed, while others are in imminent danger of being crossed.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rockström and Steffen collaborated with 26 leading academics, including <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel</a> laureate <a href="/wiki/Paul_Crutzen" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul Crutzen">Paul Crutzen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Institute_for_Space_Studies" title="Goddard Institute for Space Studies">Goddard Institute for Space Studies</a> climate scientist <a href="/wiki/James_Hansen" title="James Hansen">James Hansen</a> and the <a href="/wiki/German_Chancellor" class="mw-redirect" title="German Chancellor">German Chancellor</a>'s chief climate adviser <a href="/wiki/Hans_Joachim_Schellnhuber" title="Hans Joachim Schellnhuber">Hans Joachim Schellnhuber</a>. The group identified nine "planetary life support systems" essential for human survival, and attempted to quantify just how far seven of these systems have been pushed already. They then estimated how much further we can go before our own survival is threatened; beyond these boundaries there is a risk of "irreversible and abrupt environmental change" which could make Earth less <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">habitable</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rockstr.C3.B6m_2009_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rockstr.C3.B6m_2009-1">[1]</a></sup> Estimates indicate that three of these boundaries—<a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, biodiversity loss, and the biogeochemical flow boundary—appear to have been crossed. The boundaries are "rough, first estimates only, surrounded by large uncertainties and knowledge gaps" that interact in ways that are complex and not well understood. Boundaries can help identify where there is room and define a "safe space for human development", which is an improvement on approaches which aim at just minimizing <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">human impacts</a> on the planet.<sup id="cite_ref-Rockstr.C3.B6m_2009_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rockstr.C3.B6m_2009-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The group published their full findings in a 2009 report<sup id="cite_ref-Rockstr.C3.B6m_2009_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rockstr.C3.B6m_2009-1">[1]</a></sup> and presented it to the General Assembly of the <a href="/wiki/Club_of_Rome" title="Club of Rome">Club of Rome</a> in Amsterdam.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> An edited summary of the report was subsequently published as the featured article in a special edition of <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> <i>Nature</i> also published critical commentary from leading academics they invited to comment on each of the seven planetary boundaries which had been quantified, including comments from Nobel laureate <a href="/wiki/Mario_J._Molina" title="Mario J. Molina">Mario J. Molina</a> and biologist <a href="/wiki/Cristi%C3%A1n_Samper" title="Cristián Samper">Cristián Samper</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_idea_of_planetary_boundaries_or_limits">The idea of planetary boundaries or limits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: The idea of planetary boundaries or limits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Terra.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Terra.png/220px-Terra.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Terra.png/330px-Terra.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Terra.png/440px-Terra.png 2x" data-file-width="599" data-file-height="599" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Terra.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>The planet Earth is a finite system, which means it has limits</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The idea that our planet has limits, including the burden placed upon it by human activities, has been around for some time. In 1972, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Limits_to_Growth" title="The Limits to Growth">The Limits to Growth</a></i> was published. It presented a model in which five variables: <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a>, industrialization, pollution, food production, and <a href="/wiki/Resources_depletion" class="mw-redirect" title="Resources depletion">resources depletion</a>, are examined, and considered to grow <a href="/wiki/Exponential_growth" title="Exponential growth">exponentially</a>, whereas the ability of technology to increase resources availability is only <a href="/wiki/Linear_function" title="Linear function">linear</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Subsequently, the report was widely dismissed, particularly by economists and businessmen,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> and it has often been claimed that history has proved the projections to be incorrect.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> In 2008, Graham Turner from the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Scientific_and_Industrial_Research_Organisation" title="Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation">Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation</a> (CSIRO) published "A comparison of <i>The Limits to Growth</i> with thirty years of reality".<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2008_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner_2008-11">[11]</a></sup> Turner found that the observed historical data from 1970 to 2000 closely matches the simulated results of the "standard run" limits of growth model for almost all the outputs reported. "The comparison is well within uncertainty bounds of nearly all the data in terms of both magnitude and the trends over time."<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2008_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner_2008-11">[11]</a></sup> Turner also examined a number of reports, particularly by economists, which over the years have purported to discredit the limits-to-growth model. Turner says these reports are flawed, and reflect misunderstandings about the model.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2008_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner_2008-11">[11]</a></sup> In 2010, Nørgård, Peet and Ragnarsdóttir called the book a "pioneering report", and said that it "has withstood the test of time and, indeed, has only become more relevant."<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #f8f8f8;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>With few exceptions, economics as a discipline has been dominated by a perception of living in an unlimited world, where resource and pollution problems in one area were solved by moving resources or people to other parts. The very hint of any global limitation as suggested in the report <i>The Limits to Growth</i> was met with disbelief and rejection by businesses and most economists. However, this conclusion was mostly based on false premises.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: right;">– <a href="#CITEREFMeyerN.C3.B8rg.C3.A5rd2010">Meyer &amp; Nørgård (2010)</a>.</cite></div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Our_Common_Future" title="Our Common Future">Our Common Future</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> was published in 1987 by United Nations' <a href="/wiki/Brundtland_Commission" title="Brundtland Commission">World Commission on Environment and Development</a>. It tried to recapture the spirit of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Conference_on_the_Human_Environment" title="United Nations Conference on the Human Environment">Stockholm Conference</a>. Its aim was to interlock the concepts of development and environment for future political discussions. It introduced the famous definition for <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">sustainable development</a>:</p>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">development that meets the needs of the present without compromising the ability of future generations to meet their own needs.</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3" class="cquotecite" style="border: none; padding-right: 4%; font-size: smaller; text-align: right;"><cite>— <i><a href="#CITEREFBrundtland_Report1987">Brundtland Report 1987</a></i></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Of a different kind is the approach made by <a href="/wiki/James_Lovelock" title="James Lovelock">James Lovelock</a>. In the 1970s he and <a href="/wiki/Microbiologist" title="Microbiologist">microbiologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Lynn_Margulis" title="Lynn Margulis">Lynn Margulis</a> presented the <a href="/wiki/Gaia_hypothesis" title="Gaia hypothesis">Gaia theory or hypothesis</a>, that states that all <a href="/wiki/Organisms" class="mw-redirect" title="Organisms">organisms</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Inorganic" class="mw-redirect" title="Inorganic">inorganic</a> surroundings on Earth are integrated into a single self-regulating system.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The system has the ability to react to perturbations or deviations, much like a living organism adjusts its regulation mechanisms to accommodate environmental changes such as temperature (<a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a>). Nevertheless, this capacity has limits. For instance, when a living organism is subjected to a temperature that is lower or higher than its living range, it can perish because its regulating mechanism cannot make the necessary adjustments. Similarly the Earth may not be able to react to large deviations in critical parameters. In his book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Revenge_of_Gaia" title="The Revenge of Gaia">The Revenge of Gaia</a></i>, he affirms that the destruction of rainforests and biodiversity, compounded with the increase of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> made by humans, is producing <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="From_Holocene_to_Anthropocene">From Holocene to Anthropocene</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: From Holocene to Anthropocene">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #f8f8f8;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>Our planet’s ability to provide an accommodating environment for humanity is being challenged by our own activities. The environment—our life-support system—is changing rapidly from the stable Holocene state of the last 12,000 years, during which we developed agriculture, villages, cities, and contemporary civilizations, to an unknown future state of significantly different conditions.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: right;">– <a href="#CITEREFSteffenRockstr.C3.B6mCostanza2011">Steffen, Rockström &amp; Costanza (2011)</a></cite></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> began about 10,000 years ago. It is the current <a href="/wiki/Interglacial" title="Interglacial">interglacial</a> period, and it has proven to be a relatively stable environment of the Earth. There have been natural environmental fluctuations during the Holocene, but the key atmospheric and biogeochemical parameters have been relatively stable.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> This stability and <a href="/wiki/Resilience_(ecology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Resilience (ecology)">resilience</a> has allowed agriculture to develop and complex societies to thrive.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> According to Rockström <i>et al.</i>, we "have now become so dependent on those investments for our way of life, and how we have organized society, technologies, and economies around them, that we must take the range within which Earth System processes varied in the Holocene as a scientific reference point for a desirable planetary state."<sup id="cite_ref-Rockstr.C3.B6m_2009_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rockstr.C3.B6m_2009-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 300px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em">
<caption>External image</caption>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/16px-Searchtool.svg.png" width="16" height="16" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/24px-Searchtool.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/32px-Searchtool.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512" /> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ecologyandsociety.org/vol14/iss2/art32/figure1.html">The last glacial cycle of δ<sup>18</sup>O</a> indicative of the stability of the Holocene over the last 10,000 years<br />
–&#160;Adapted from <a href="#CITEREFYoungSteffen2009">Young &amp; Steffen (2009)</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a>, according to <a href="/wiki/Paul_J._Crutzen" title="Paul J. Crutzen">Paul Crutzen</a>, Will Steffen and others, the planet has entered a new epoch, the <a href="/wiki/Anthropocene" title="Anthropocene">Anthropocene</a>. In the Anthropocene, humans have become the main agents of change to the Earth system.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> There have been well publicized scientific warnings about risks in the areas of climate change and stratospheric ozone.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> However, other <a href="/wiki/Biophysical" class="mw-redirect" title="Biophysical">biophysical</a> processes are also important.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> For example, since the advent of the Anthropocene, the rate at which species are being extinguished has increased over 100 times,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> and humans are now the driving force altering global river flows<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> as well as water vapor flows from the land surface.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Continuing pressure on the Earth's biophysical systems from human activities raises concerns that further pressure could be destabilizing, and precipitate sudden or irreversible changes to the environment. It is difficult to address the issue, because the predominant paradigms of social and economic development are largely indifferent to the looming possibilities of large scale environmental disasters triggered by humans.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_nine_boundaries">The nine boundaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: The nine boundaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><b>Thresholds and boundaries</b></p>
<p>The <i>threshold</i>, or climatological <a href="/wiki/Tipping_point_(climatology)" title="Tipping point (climatology)">tipping point</a>, is the value at which a very small increment for the control variable (like CO<sub>2</sub>) produces a large, possibly catastrophic, change in the response variable (global warming).</p>
<p>The threshold points are difficult to locate, because the Earth System is very complex. Instead of defining the threshold value, the study establishes a range, and the threshold is supposed to lie inside it. The lower end of that range is defined as the <i>boundary</i>. Therefore, it defines a safe space, in the sense that as long as we are below the boundary, we are below the threshold value. If the boundary is crossed, we enter into a danger zone.<sup id="cite_ref-Rockstr.C3.B6m_2009_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rockstr.C3.B6m_2009-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
