<p>The proposed framework of planetary boundaries lays the groundwork for shifting approach to governance and management, away from the essentially sectoral analyses of limits to growth aimed at minimizing negative <a href="/wiki/Externality" title="Externality">externalities</a>, toward the estimation of the safe space for <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">human development</a>. Planetary boundaries define, as it were, the boundaries of the “planetary playing field” for humanity if major <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">human-induced environmental change</a> on a global scale is to be avoided</p>
<p>Transgressing one or more planetary boundaries may be highly damaging or even catastrophic, due to the risk of crossing thresholds that trigger non-linear, abrupt <a href="/wiki/Environmental_change" title="Environmental change">environmental change</a> within <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continental</a>- to planetary-scale systems. The 2009 study identified nine planetary boundaries and, drawing on current scientific understanding, the researchers proposed quantifications for seven of them. These seven are climate change (<a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a> concentration in the atmosphere &lt; 350 ppm and/or a maximum change of +1 W/m<sup>2</sup> in <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">radiative forcing</a>); <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a> (mean surface seawater saturation state with respect to aragonite ≥ 80% of pre-<a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">industrial</a> levels); <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratospheric</a> ozone (less than 5% reduction in total atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">O<sub>3</sub></a> from a pre-industrial level of 290 <a href="/wiki/Dobson_Unit" class="mw-redirect" title="Dobson Unit">Dobson Units</a>); <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle" title="Biogeochemical cycle">biogeochemical</a> <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_cycle" title="Nitrogen cycle">nitrogen (N) cycle</a> (limit industrial and agricultural fixation of N<sub>2</sub> to 35 Tg N/yr) and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_cycle" title="Phosphorus cycle">phosphorus (P) cycle</a> (annual P inflow to oceans not to exceed 10 times the natural background <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a> of P); global freshwater use (&lt; 4000&#160;km<sup>3</sup>/yr of consumptive use of runoff resources); land system change (&lt; 15% of the ice-free land surface under cropland); and the rate at which biological diversity is lost (annual rate of &lt; 10 extinctions per million species). The two additional planetary boundaries for which the group had not yet been able to determine a boundary level are <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">chemical pollution and atmospheric aerosol loading</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Debate_on_the_framework">Debate on the framework</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Debate on the framework">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #f8f8f8;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<center><b>From the <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_Memorandum" title="Stockholm Memorandum">Stockholm Memorandum</a></b></center>
Science indicates that we are transgressing planetary boundaries that have kept civilization safe for the past 10,000 years. Evidence is growing that human pressures are starting to overwhelm the Earth’s buffering capacity. Humans are now the most significant driver of <a href="/wiki/Global_change" title="Global change">global change</a>, propelling the planet into a new geological epoch, the <a href="/wiki/Anthropocene" title="Anthropocene">Anthropocene</a>. We can no longer exclude the possibility that our collective actions will trigger tipping points, risking abrupt and irreversible consequences for human communities and ecological systems.</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: right;">– <a href="#CITEREFStockholm_Memorandum2011">Stockholm Memorandum (2011)</a></cite></div>
<p>The framework of planetary boundaries and the associated planetary safety zone is new and evolving. It is drawing strong responses from scientists and advisors.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christopher_Field" title="Christopher Field">Christopher Field</a>, director of the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Institution" class="mw-redirect" title="Carnegie Institution">Carnegie Institution's Department of Global Ecology</a>, is impressed: "This kind of work is critically important. Overall, this is an impressive attempt to define a safety zone."<sup id="cite_ref-Zimmer_2009_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zimmer_2009-38">[38]</a></sup> But the conservation biologist <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Pimm" title="Stuart Pimm">Stuart Pimm</a> is not impressed: "I don’t think this is in any way a useful way of thinking about things… The notion of a single boundary is just devoid of serious content. In what way is an extinction rate 10 times the background rate acceptable?"<sup id="cite_ref-Zimmer_2009_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zimmer_2009-38">[38]</a></sup> and the environmental policy analyst <a href="/wiki/William_C._Clark" title="William C. Clark">Bill Clark</a> thinks: "Tipping points in the earth system are dense, unpredictable... and unlikely to be avoidable through early warning indicators. It follows that... 'safe operating spaces' and 'planetary boundaries' are thus highly suspect and potentially the new 'opiates'."<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>The biogeochemist <a href="/wiki/William_H._Schlesinger" title="William H. Schlesinger">William Schlesinger</a> queries whether thresholds are a good idea for pollutions at all. He thinks waiting until we near some suggested limit will just permit us to continue to a point where it is too late. "Management based on thresholds, although attractive in its simplicity, allows pernicious, slow and diffuse degradation to persist nearly indefinitely."<sup id="cite_ref-Schlesinger_2009_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlesinger_2009-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>The hydrologist David Molden thinks planetary boundaries are a welcome new approach in the 'limits to growth' debate. "As a scientific organizing principle, the concept has many strengths [...] the numbers are important because they provide targets for policymakers, giving a clear indication of the magnitude and direction of change. They also provide benchmarks and direction for science. As we improve our understanding of Earth processes and complex inter-relationships, these benchmarks can and will be updated [...] we now have a tool we can use to help us think more deeply—and urgently—about planetary limits and the critical actions we have to take."<sup id="cite_ref-Molden_2009_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Molden_2009-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ocean_chemist" class="mw-redirect" title="Ocean chemist">ocean chemist</a> Peter Brewer queries whether it is "truly useful to create a list of environmental limits without serious plans for how they may be achieved [...] they may become just another stick to beat citizens with. Disruption of the global nitrogen cycle is one clear example: it is likely that a large fraction of people on Earth would not be alive today without the artificial production of fertilizer. How can such ethical and economic issues be matched with a simple call to set limits? [...] food is not optional."<sup id="cite_ref-Brewer_2009_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brewer_2009-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The environment advisor Steve Bass says the "description of planetary boundaries is a sound idea. We need to know how to live within the unusually stable conditions of our present Holocene period and not do anything that causes irreversible environmental change […] Their paper has profound implications for future governance systems, offering some of the 'wiring' needed to link governance of national and global economies with governance of the environment and natural resources. The planetary boundaries concept should enable policymakers to understand more clearly that, like human rights and representative government, environmental change knows no borders."<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The climate change policy advisor Adele Morris thinks that price-based policies are also needed to avoid political and economic thresholds. "Staying within a 'safe operating space' will require staying within all the relevant boundaries, including the electorate’s willingness to pay."<sup id="cite_ref-Morris_2010_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morris_2010-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #f8f8f8;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>In summary, the planetary boundary concept is a very important one, and its proposal should now be followed by discussions of the connections between the various boundaries and of their association with other concepts such as the '<a href="/wiki/Limits_to_growth" class="mw-redirect" title="Limits to growth">limits to growth</a>'. Importantly, this novel concept highlights the risk of reaching thresholds or tipping points for non-linear or abrupt changes in Earth-system processes. As such, it can help society to reach the agreements required for dealing effectively with existing global environmental threats, such as climate change.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: right;">– Nobel laureate <a href="/wiki/Mario_J._Molina" title="Mario J. Molina">Mario&#160;J.&#160;Molina</a><sup id="cite_ref-Molina_2009_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Molina_2009-45">[45]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>In their report (2012) entitled "Resilient People, Resilient Planet: A future worth choosing," The High-level Panel on Global Sustainability called for bold global efforts, "including launching a major global scientific initiative, to strengthen the interface between science and policy. We must define, through science, what scientists refer to as “planetary boundaries”, “environmental thresholds” and “tipping points”."<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2011, at their second meeting, the High-level Panel on Global Sustainability<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> of the United Nations had incorporated the concept of planetary boundaries into their framework, stating that their goal was: "To eradicate poverty and reduce inequality, make growth inclusive, and production and consumption more sustainable while combating climate change and respecting the range of other planetary boundaries."<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Elsewhere in their proceedings, panel members have expressed reservations about the political effectiveness of using the concept of "planetary boundaries"&#160;: "Planetary boundaries are still an evolving concept that should be used with caution […] The planetary boundaries question can be divisive as it can be perceived as a tool of the “North” to tell the “South” not to follow the resource intensive and environmentally destructive development pathway that rich countries took themselves… This language is unacceptable to most of the developing countries as they fear that an emphasis on boundaries would place unacceptable brakes on poor countries."<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, the concept is routinely used in the proceedings of the United Nations,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> and in the <i>UN Daily News</i>. For example, the <a href="/wiki/UNEP" class="mw-redirect" title="UNEP">UNEP</a> Executive Director <a href="/wiki/Achim_Steiner" title="Achim Steiner">Achim Steiner</a> states that the challenge of agriculture is to "feed a growing global population without pushing humanity's <a href="/wiki/Ecological_footprint" title="Ecological footprint">footprint</a> beyond planetary boundaries."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a> (UNEP) Yearbook 2010 also repeated Rockström's message, conceptually linking it with <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_management" title="Ecosystem management">ecosystem management</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Governance_Indicators" title="Sustainable Governance Indicators">environmental governance indicators</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>The planetary boundaries concept is also used in proceedings by the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and was referred to in the <a href="/wiki/European_Environment_Agency" title="European Environment Agency">European Environment Agency</a> synthesis report <i>The European environment – state and outlook 2010</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Debate_on_the_boundaries">Debate on the boundaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Debate on the boundaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There is debate about which boundaries are most relevant, and how or whether they can be usefully quantified.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_and_global_annual_average_temperatures_over_the_years_1880_to_2009.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_and_global_annual_average_temperatures_over_the_years_1880_to_2009.png/300px-Atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_and_global_annual_average_temperatures_over_the_years_1880_to_2009.png" width="300" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_and_global_annual_average_temperatures_over_the_years_1880_to_2009.png/450px-Atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_and_global_annual_average_temperatures_over_the_years_1880_to_2009.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_and_global_annual_average_temperatures_over_the_years_1880_to_2009.png/600px-Atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_and_global_annual_average_temperatures_over_the_years_1880_to_2009.png 2x" data-file-width="1198" data-file-height="956" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_and_global_annual_average_temperatures_over_the_years_1880_to_2009.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The black line shows the atmospheric carbon dioxide concentration for the period 1880–2008. Red bars show temperatures above and blue bars show temperatures below the average temperature. Year-to-year temperature fluctuations are due to natural processes, such as the effects of <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Niño">El Niño</a>, <a href="/wiki/La_Ni%C3%B1a" title="La Niña">La Niña</a>, and the eruption of large volcanoes.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">Radiative forcing</a> is a measure of the difference between the incoming <a href="/wiki/Irradiance" title="Irradiance">radiation energy</a> and the outgoing radiation energy acting across the boundary of the earth. Positive radiative forcing results in warming. From the start of the industrial revolution in 1750 to 2005, the increase in atmospheric carbon dioxide has led to a positive radiative forcing, averaging about 1.66&#160;W/m².<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>The climate scientist <a href="/wiki/Myles_Allen" title="Myles Allen">Myles Allen</a> thinks setting "a limit on long-term atmospheric carbon dioxide concentrations merely distracts from the much more immediate challenge of limiting warming to 2 °C." He says the concentration of carbon dioxide is not a control variable we can "meaningfully claim to control", and he questions whether keeping carbon dioxide levels below 350 ppm will avoid more than 2&#160;°C of warming.<sup id="cite_ref-Allen_2009_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allen_2009-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Adele Morris, policy director, Climate and Energy Economics Project, <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a>, makes a criticism from the economical-political point of view. She puts emphasis in choosing policies that minimize costs and preserve consensus. She favors a system of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">green-house gas emissions tax</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Emissions_trading" title="Emissions trading">emissions trading</a>, as ways to prevent global warming. She thinks that too-ambitious objectives, like the boundary limit on CO<sub>2</sub>, may discourage such actions.<sup id="cite_ref-Morris_2010_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morris_2010-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity_loss">Biodiversity loss</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Biodiversity loss">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the biologist <a href="/wiki/Cristi%C3%A1n_Samper" title="Cristián Samper">Cristián Samper</a>, a " boundary that expresses the probability of families of species disappearing over time would better reflect our potential impacts on the future of life on Earth."<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>The conservation ecologist Gretchen Daily claims that "it is time to confront the hard truth that traditional approaches to conservation, taken alone, are doomed to fail. Nature reserves are too small, too few, too isolated and too subject to change to support more than a tiny fraction of Earth’s biodiversity. The challenge is to make conservation attractive—from economic and cultural perspectives. We cannot go on treating nature like an all-you-can-eat buffet. We depend on nature for food security, clean water, climate stability, seafood, timber, and other biological and physical services. To maintain these benefits, we need not just remote reserves but places everywhere—more like 'ecosystem service stations.' A few pioneers are integrating conservation and human development. The Costa Rican government is paying landowners for ecosystem services from tropical forests, including carbon offsets, hydropower production, biodiversity conservation and scenic beauty. <a href="/wiki/PRC" class="mw-redirect" title="PRC">China</a> is investing $100 billion in “ecocompensation,” including innovative policy and finance mechanisms that reward conservation and restoration. The country is also creating “ecosystem function conservation areas” that make up 18 percent of its land area. Colombia and South Africa have made dramatic policy changes, too. Three advances would help the rest of the world scale such models of success. One: new science and tools to value and account for natural capital, in biophysical, economic and other terms [...] Two: compelling demonstrations of such tools in resource policy. Three: cooperation among governments, development organizations, corporations and communities to help nations build more durable economies while also maintaining critical ecosystem services."<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biogeochemical">Biogeochemical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Biogeochemical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:382px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nitrogen_Cycle.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Nitrogen_Cycle.svg/380px-Nitrogen_Cycle.svg.png" width="380" height="285" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Nitrogen_Cycle.svg/570px-Nitrogen_Cycle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Nitrogen_Cycle.svg/760px-Nitrogen_Cycle.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nitrogen_Cycle.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_cycle" title="Nitrogen cycle">Nitrogen cycle</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nitrogen_cycle">Nitrogen cycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nitrogen cycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Since the industrial revolution, the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_cycle" title="Nitrogen cycle">nitrogen cycle</a> has been disturbed even more than the carbon cycle. "Human activities now convert more nitrogen from the atmosphere into reactive forms than all of the Earth´s terrestrial processes combined. Much of this new reactive nitrogen pollutes waterways and coastal zones, is emitted back to the atmosphere in changed forms, or accumulates in the terrestrial biosphere."<sup id="cite_ref-Stockholm_Resilience_Centre_2009_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stockholm_Resilience_Centre_2009-59">[59]</a></sup> Only a small part of the fertilizers applied in agriculture is used by plants. Most of the nitrogen and phosphorus ends up in rivers, lakes and the sea, where excess amounts stress aquatic ecosystems. For example, fertilizer which discharges from rivers into the Gulf of Mexico has damaged <a href="/wiki/Shrimp_fishery" title="Shrimp fishery">shrimp fisheries</a> because of <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(environmental)" title="Hypoxia (environmental)">hypoxia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stockholm_Resilience_Centre_2009_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stockholm_Resilience_Centre_2009-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>The biogeochemist <a href="/wiki/William_H._Schlesinger" title="William H. Schlesinger">William Schlesinger</a> thinks waiting until we near some suggested limit for nitrogen deposition and other pollutions will just permit us to continue to a point where it is too late. He says the boundary suggested for phosphorus is not sustainable, and would exhaust the known phosphorus reserves in less than 200 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Schlesinger_2009_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlesinger_2009-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>With regard to nitrogen, the biogeochemist and ecosystem scientist Robert Howarth says: "Human activity has greatly altered the flow of nitrogen across the globe. The single largest contributor is fertilizer use. But the burning of fossil fuels actually dominates the problem in some regions, such as the northeastern U.S. The solution in that case is to conserve energy and use it more efficiently. Hybrid vehicles are another excellent fix; their nitrogen emissions are significantly less than traditional vehicles because their engines turn off while the vehicle is stopped. (Emissions from conventional vehicles actually rise when the engine is idling.) Nitrogen emissions from U.S. power plants could be greatly reduced, too, if plants that predate the Clean Air Act and its amendments were required to comply; these plants pollute far out of proportion to the amount of electricity they produce.</p>
<p>In agriculture, many farmers could use less fertilizer, and the reductions in crop yields would be small or nonexistent. Runoff from corn fields is particularly avoidable because corn’s roots penetrate only the top few inches of soil and assimilate nutrients for only two months of the year. In addition, nitrogen losses can be reduced by 30 percent or more if farmers plant winter cover crops, such as rye or wheat, which can help the soil hold nitrogen. These crops also increase carbon sequestration in soils, mitigating climate change. Better yet is to grow perennial plants such as grasses rather than corn; nitrogen losses are many times lower. Nitrogen pollution from <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_Animal_Feeding_Operations" class="mw-redirect" title="Concentrated Animal Feeding Operations">concentrated animal feeding operations</a> (CAFOs) is a huge problem.</p>
<p>As recently as the 1970s, most animals were fed local crops, and the animals’ wastes were returned to the fields as fertilizer. Today most U.S. animals are fed crops grown hundreds of miles away, making it “uneconomical” to return the manure. The solution? Require CAFO owners to treat their wastes, just as municipalities must do with human wastes. Further, if we ate less meat, less waste would be generated and less synthetic fertilizer would be needed to grow animal feed. Eating meat from animals that are range-fed on perennial grasses would be ideal. The explosive growth in the production of ethanol as a biofuel is greatly aggravating nitrogen pollution. Several studies have suggested that if mandated U.S. ethanol targets are met, the amount of nitrogen flowing down the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> and fueling the Gulf of Mexico dead zone may increase by 30 to 40 percent. The best alternative would be to forgo the production of ethanol from corn. If the country wants to rely on biofuels, it should instead grow grasses and trees and burn these to co-generate heat and electricity; nitrogen pollution and greenhouse gas emissions would be much lower."<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Phosphorus">Phosphorus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Phosphorus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Peak_phosphorus" title="Peak phosphorus">Peak phosphorus</a></div>
<p>With regard to phosphorus, the ocean engineer David Vaccari says that the most sustainable environmental <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_cycle" title="Phosphorus cycle">flow of phosphorus</a> "would be the natural flux: seven million metric tons per year (Mt/yr). To hit that mark yet satisfy our usage of 22 Mt/yr, we would have to recycle or reuse 72 percent of our phosphorus […] The flow could be reduced with existing technologies… [lowering] the loss to waterways from 22 to 8.25 Mt/yr, not very much above the natural flux."<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Peak phosphorus is a concept to describe the point in time at which the maximum global <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> production rate is reached. Phosphorus is a scarce finite resource on earth and means of production other than mining are unavailable because of its non-gaseous environmental cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> According to some researchers, Earth's phosphorus reserves are expected to be completely depleted in 50–100 years and peak phosphorus to be reached in approximately 2030.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AYool_GLODAP_del_pH.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/AYool_GLODAP_del_pH.png/300px-AYool_GLODAP_del_pH.png" width="300" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/AYool_GLODAP_del_pH.png/450px-AYool_GLODAP_del_pH.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/AYool_GLODAP_del_pH.png/600px-AYool_GLODAP_del_pH.png 2x" data-file-width="1683" data-file-height="1133" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AYool_GLODAP_del_pH.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Estimated change in sea surface pH from the pre-industrial period (1700s) to the present day (1990s). Δ pH is in standard pH units.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ocean_acidification">Ocean acidification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ocean acidification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">Surface ocean acidity</a> has increased thirty percent since the industrial revolution. About one quarter of the additional carbon dioxide generated by humans is dissolved in the oceans, where it forms <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a>. This acidity inhibits the ability of corals, shellfish and plankton to build shells and skeletons. <a href="/wiki/Cascade_effect_(ecology)" title="Cascade effect (ecology)">Knock-on effects</a> could have serious consequences for <a href="/wiki/Fish_stock" title="Fish stock">fish stocks</a>. This boundary is clearly interconnected with the climate change boundaries, since the concentration of carbon dioxide in the atmosphere is also the underlying control variable for the ocean acidification boundary.<sup id="cite_ref-Stockholm_Resilience_Centre_2009_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stockholm_Resilience_Centre_2009-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ocean chemist Peter Brewer thinks "ocean acidification has impacts other than simple changes in pH, and these may need boundaries too."<sup id="cite_ref-Brewer_2009_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brewer_2009-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The marine chemist Scott Doney thinks "the main tactics are raising energy efficiency, switching to renewable and nuclear power, protecting forests and exploring carbon sequestration technologies. Regionally, nutrient runoff to coastal waters not only creates dead zones but also amplifies acidification. The excess nutrients cause more phytoplankton to grow, and as they die the added CO2 from their decay acidifies the water. We have to be smarter about how we fertilize fields and lawns and treat livestock manure and sewage […] Locally, acidic water could be buffered with limestone or chemical bases produced electrochemically from seawater and rocks. More practical may be protecting specific shellfish beds and aquaculture fisheries. Larval mollusks such as clams and oysters appear to be more susceptible to acidification than adults, and recycling old clamshells into the mud may help buffer pH and provide better substrate for larval attachment. The drop in ocean pH is expected to accelerate in coming decades, so marine ecosystems will have to adapt. We can enhance their chances for success by reducing other insults such as water pollution and overfishing, making them better able to withstand some acidification while we transition away from a fossil-fuel energy economy."<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_use">Land use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Land use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe_land_use_map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Europe_land_use_map.png/350px-Europe_land_use_map.png" width="350" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Europe_land_use_map.png/525px-Europe_land_use_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Europe_land_use_map.png/700px-Europe_land_use_map.png 2x" data-file-width="1004" data-file-height="740" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe_land_use_map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Europe land use map. Human land uses include arable farmland (yellow) and pasture (light green)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Across the planet, forests, wetlands and other vegetation types are being converted to agricultural and other <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">land uses</a>, impacting freshwater, carbon and other cycles, and reducing biodiversity.<sup id="cite_ref-Stockholm_Resilience_Centre_2009_59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stockholm_Resilience_Centre_2009-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>The environment advisor Steve Bass says research tells us that "the sustainability of land use depends less on percentages and more on other factors. For example, the environmental impact of 15 per cent coverage by intensively farmed cropland in large blocks will be significantly different from that of 15 per cent of land farmed in more sustainable ways, integrated into the landscape. The boundary of 15 per cent land-use change is, in practice, a premature policy guideline that dilutes the authors' overall scientific proposition. Instead, the authors might want to consider a limit on soil degradation or soil loss. This would be a more valid and useful indicator of the state of terrestrial health."<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Earth systems scientist Eric Lambin thinks that "intensive agriculture should be concentrated on land that has the best potential for high-yield crops […] We can avoid losing the best agricultural land by controlling land degradation, freshwater depletion and urban sprawl. This step will require zoning and the adoption of more efficient agricultural practices, especially in developing countries. The need for farmland can be lessened, too, by decreasing waste along the food distribution chain, encouraging slower population growth, ensuring more equitable food distribution worldwide and significantly reducing meat consumption in rich countries."<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Freshwater">Freshwater</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Freshwater">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PeakWaterAquifer.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/PeakWaterAquifer.PNG/300px-PeakWaterAquifer.PNG" width="300" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c6/PeakWaterAquifer.PNG 1.5x" data-file-width="433" data-file-height="222" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PeakWaterAquifer.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Overexploitation of <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> from an <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifer</a> can result in a <a href="/wiki/Peak_water" title="Peak water">peak water</a> curve.<sup id="cite_ref-Palaniappan_2008_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palaniappan_2008-69">[69]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Human pressures on global <a href="/wiki/Freshwater" class="mw-redirect" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a> systems are having <a href="/wiki/Water_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Water crisis">dramatic effects</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">freshwater cycle</a> is another boundary significantly affected by climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-Stockholm_Resilience_Centre_2009_59-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stockholm_Resilience_Centre_2009-59">[59]</a></sup> Freshwater resources, such as lakes and <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a>, are usually renewable resources which naturally recharge (the term <a href="/wiki/Fossil_water" title="Fossil water">fossil water</a> is sometimes used to describe aquifers which don't recharge). <a href="/wiki/Overexploitation" title="Overexploitation">Overexploitation</a> occurs if a water resource is mined or extracted at a rate that exceeds the recharge rate. Recharge usually comes from area streams, rivers and lakes. Forests enhance the recharge of aquifers in some locales, although generally forests are a major source of aquifer <a href="/wiki/Overdrafting" title="Overdrafting">depletion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Depleted aquifers can become polluted with contaminants such as <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrates</a>, or permanently damaged through subsidence or through saline intrusion from the ocean. This turns much of the world's underground water and lakes into finite resources with peak usage debates similar to <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">oil</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Though Hubbert's original analysis did not apply to renewable resources, their overexploitation can result in a <a href="/wiki/Hubbert_peak" class="mw-redirect" title="Hubbert peak">Hubbert-like peak</a>. A modified <a href="/wiki/Hubbert_curve" title="Hubbert curve">Hubbert curve</a> applies to any resource that can be harvested faster than it can be replaced.<sup id="cite_ref-Palaniappan_2008_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palaniappan_2008-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hydrologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrologist">hydrologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Gleick" title="Peter Gleick">Peter Gleick</a> comments: "Few rational observers deny the need for boundaries to freshwater use. More controversial is defining where those limits are or what steps to take to constrain ourselves within them. Another way to describe these boundaries is the concept of <a href="/wiki/Peak_water" title="Peak water">peak water</a>. Three different ideas are useful. 'Peak renewable' water limits are the total renewable flows in a watershed. Many of the world’s major rivers are already approaching this threshold—when evaporation and consumption surpass natural replenishment from precipitation and other sources. 'Peak nonrenewable' limits apply where human use of water far exceeds natural recharge rates, such as in fossil groundwater basins of the Great Plains, Libya, India, northern China and parts of California’s Central Valley. 'Peak ecological' water is the idea that for any hydrological system, increasing withdrawals eventually reach the point where any additional economic benefit of taking the water is outweighed by the additional ecological destruction that causes. Although it is difficult to quantify this point accurately, we have clearly passed the point of peak ecological water in many basins around the world where huge damage has occurred […] The good news is that the potential for savings, without hurting human health or economic productivity, is vast. Improvements in water-use efficiency are possible in every sector. More food can be grown with less water (and less water contamination) by shifting from conventional flood irrigation to drip and precision sprinklers, along with more accurately monitoring and managing soil moisture. Conventional power plants can change from water cooling to dry cooling, and more energy can be generated by sources that use extremely little water, such as photovoltaics and wind."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>The hydrologist David Molden says "a global limit on water consumption is necessary, but the suggested planetary boundary of 4,000 cubic kilometres per year is too generous."<sup id="cite_ref-Molden_2009_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Molden_2009-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ozone_depletion">Ozone depletion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Ozone depletion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">Ozone depletion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png/220px-NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png/330px-NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png/440px-NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png 2x" data-file-width="716" data-file-height="716" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
During 21–30 September 2006 the average area of the Antarctic ozone hole was the largest ever observed</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The stratospheric <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a> protectively filters <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Ultraviolet radiation">ultraviolet radiation</a> (UV) from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, which would otherwise damage biological systems. The actions taken after the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Protocol" title="Montreal Protocol">Montreal Protocol</a> appeared to be keeping the planet within a safe boundary.<sup id="cite_ref-Stockholm_Resilience_Centre_2009_59-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stockholm_Resilience_Centre_2009-59">[59]</a></sup> However, in 2011, according to a paper published in <i>Nature</i>, the boundary was unexpectedly <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion#Arctic_ozone_hole" title="Ozone depletion">pushed in the Arctic</a>; "... the fraction of the Arctic vortex in March with total ozone less than 275 Dobson units (DU) is typically near zero, but reached nearly 45%".<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Nobel laureate in chemistry, <a href="/wiki/Mario_J._Molina" title="Mario J. Molina">Mario Molina</a>, says "five per cent is a reasonable limit for acceptable ozone depletion, but it doesn't represent a tipping point".<sup id="cite_ref-Molina_2009_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Molina_2009-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>The physicist David Fahey says that as a result of the Montreal Protocol "stratospheric ozone depletion will largely reverse by 2100. The gain has relied, in part, on intermediate substitutes, notably hydrochlorofluorocarbons (HCFCs), and the growing use of compounds that cause no depletion, such as hydrofluorocarbons (HFCs). Ongoing success depends on several steps:</p>
<ul>
<li>"Continue observing the ozone layer to promptly reveal unexpected changes. Ensure that nations adhere to regulations; for example, the HCFC phaseout will not be complete until 2030.</li>
<li>"Maintain the Scientific Assessment Panel under the protocol. It attributes causes of changes in the ozone layer and evaluates new chemicals for their potential to destroy ozone and contribute to climate change.</li>
<li>"Maintain the Technology and Economic Assessment Panel. It provides information on technologies and substitute compounds that helps nations assess how the demand for applications such as refrigeration, air-conditioning and foam insulation can be met while protecting the ozone layer.</li>
</ul>
<p>"The two panels will also have to evaluate climate change and ozone recovery together. Climate change affects ozone abundance by altering the chemical composition and dynamics of the stratosphere, and compounds such as HCFCs and HFCs are greenhouse gases. For example, the large projected demand for HFCs could significantly contribute to climate change."<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:China.A2002007.0310.250m.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/China.A2002007.0310.250m.jpg/220px-China.A2002007.0310.250m.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/China.A2002007.0310.250m.jpg/330px-China.A2002007.0310.250m.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/China.A2002007.0310.250m.jpg/440px-China.A2002007.0310.250m.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8000" data-file-height="6000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:China.A2002007.0310.250m.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Smog over southern China and Vietnam</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_aerosols">Atmospheric aerosols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Atmospheric aerosols">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">Aerosol</a> particles in the atmosphere impact the health of humans and influence <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> and global <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_circulation" title="Atmospheric circulation">atmospheric circulation</a> systems. Some aerosols produce clouds which cool the Earth by reflecting sunlight back to space, while others, like soot, produce thin clouds in the upper stratosphere which behave like a greenhouse, warming the Earth. On balance, anthropogenic aerosols probably produce a net negative <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">radiative forcing</a> (cooling influence).<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Worldwide each year, aerosol particles result in about 800,000 premature deaths. Aerosol loading is sufficiently important to be included among the planetary boundaries, but it is not yet clear whether an appropriate safe threshold measure can be identified.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_pollution">Chemical pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Chemical pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some chemicals, such as <a href="/wiki/Persistent_organic_pollutants" class="mw-redirect" title="Persistent organic pollutants">persistent organic pollutants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_(chemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Heavy metal (chemistry)">heavy metals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radionuclides</a>, have potentially irreversible <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">additive</a> and synergic effects on biological organisms, reducing fertility and resulting in permanent genetic damage. Sublethal uptakes are drastically reducing marine bird and mammal populations. This boundary seems important, although it is hard to quantify.<sup id="cite_ref-Stockholm_Resilience_Centre_2009_59-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stockholm_Resilience_Centre_2009-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>A Bayesian emulator for persistent organic pollutants has been developed which can potentially be used to quantify the boundaries for chemical pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> To date, critical exposure levels of polychlorinated biphenyls (PCBs) above which mass mortality events of marine mammals are likely to occur, have been proposed as a chemical pollution planetary boundary.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interaction_among_boundaries">Interaction among boundaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Interaction among boundaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The boundary values above were set on the assumption that interactions are not occurring if other boundaries are being crossed. However, a given planetary boundary may interact in a manner that changes the safe operating level of other boundaries. Rockström <i>et al.</i> did not analyze such interactions, but they suggested that many of these interactions will reduce rather than expand the proposed boundary levels.</p>
<p>For example, the <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">land use</a> boundary could shift downward if the freshwater boundary is breached, causing lands to become arid and unavailable for agriculture. At a regional level, water resources may decline in Asia if <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> continues in the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Rainforest" class="mw-redirect" title="Amazon Rainforest">Amazon</a>. Such considerations suggest the need for "extreme caution in approaching or transgressing any individual planetary boundaries."<sup id="cite_ref-Rockstr.C3.B6m_2009_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rockstr.C3.B6m_2009-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another example has to do with <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marine_ecosystem" title="Marine ecosystem">marine ecosystems</a>. In 2009, <a href="#CITEREFDe.27AthLoughFabricius2009">De'Ath, Lough &amp; Fabricius (2009)</a> showed that, since 1990, calcification in the reefs of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef" title="Great Barrier Reef">Great Barrier</a> that they examined decreased at a rate unprecedented over the last 400 years (14% in less than 20 years). Their evidence suggests that the increasing temperature stress and the declining ocean saturation state of <a href="/wiki/Aragonite" title="Aragonite">aragonite</a> is making it difficult for reef corals to deposit calcium carbonate. <a href="#CITEREFBellwoodothers2004">Bellwood &amp; others (2004)</a> explored how multiple stressors, such as increased nutrient loads and <a href="/wiki/Fishing_pressure" class="mw-redirect" title="Fishing pressure">fishing pressure</a>, move corals into less desirable ecosystem states. <a href="#CITEREFGuinotteFabry2008">Guinotte &amp; Fabry (2008)</a> showed that ocean acidification will significantly change the distribution and abundance of a whole range of marine life, particularly species "that build skeletons, shells, and tests of biogenic calcium carbonate. "Increasing temperatures, surface <a href="/wiki/UV_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="UV radiation">UV radiation</a> levels and ocean acidity all stress marine <a href="/wiki/Biota_(ecology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Biota (ecology)">biota</a>, and the combination of these stresses may well cause perturbations in the abundance and diversity of marine biological systems that go well beyond the effects of a single stressor acting alone."<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsequent_developments">Subsequent developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Subsequent developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #f8f8f8;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>The concept of planetary boundaries challenges the belief that resources are either limitless or infinitely substitutable. It threatens the business-as-usual approach to economic growth. The fact that reference to planetary boundaries was excluded from the [ <a href="/wiki/Rio%2B20" class="mw-redirect" title="Rio+20">Rio+20</a> ] conference statement is a counterintuitive sign that the concept is being taken very seriously and has indeed gained enough traction to be threatening to the status quo. Had planetary boundaries remained in the statement, the most credible interpretation is that they would join a growing list of nice-sounding goals that are included but never achieved in the end. Planetary boundaries will not go away. The intrinsic limits to the amount of resources and environmental services that humanity can extract safely from the Earth System cannot be eliminated by wishful thinking, denial, or omission from official sustainable development conference statements. It is simply the nature of the planet we inhabit.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: right;">– <a href="/wiki/Will_Steffen" title="Will Steffen">Will Steffen</a><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></cite></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_doughnut">The doughnut</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: The doughnut">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2012 Kate Raworth from <a href="/wiki/Oxfam" title="Oxfam">Oxfam</a> noted the Rockstrom concept does not take human population growth into account.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> She suggested social boundaries should be incorporated into the planetary boundary structure, such as jobs, education, food, access to water, health services and energy. There is a need to accommodate an environmentally safe space compatible with "poverty eradication and rights for all". Within planetary limits and an equitable social foundation lies a doughnut shaped area which is the area where there is a "safe and just space for humanity to thrive in”.<sup id="cite_ref-ITIN_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ITIN-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tenth_boundary">Tenth boundary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Tenth boundary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Also in 2012, <a href="/wiki/Steven_Running" class="mw-redirect" title="Steven Running">Steven Running</a> suggested a tenth boundary, the annual net global <a href="/wiki/Primary_production" title="Primary production">primary production</a> of all <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_plant" title="Terrestrial plant">terrestrial plants</a>, as an easily determinable measure integrating many variables that will give "a clear signal about the health of ecosystems".<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Nations_endorsement">United Nations endorsement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: United Nations endorsement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The United Nations secretary general <a href="/wiki/Ban_Ki-moon" title="Ban Ki-moon">Ban Ki-moon</a> endorsed the concept of planetary boundaries on 16 March 2012, when he presented the key points of the report of his High Level Panel on Global Sustainability to an informal <a href="/wiki/Plenary_session" title="Plenary session">plenary</a> of the UN General Assembly.<sup id="cite_ref-ITIN_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ITIN-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Ban stated: "The Panel’s vision is to eradicate poverty and reduce inequality, to make growth inclusive and production and consumption more sustainable, while combating climate change and respecting a range of other planetary boundaries."<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The concept was incorporated into the so-called "zero draft" of the outcome of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Conference_on_Sustainable_Development" title="United Nations Conference on Sustainable Development">United Nations Conference on Sustainable Development</a> to be convened in Rio de Janeiro 20–22 June 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> However, the use of the concept was subsequently withdrawn from the text of the conference, "partly due to concerns from some poorer countries that its adoption could lead to the sidelining of poverty reduction and economic development. It is also, say observers, because the idea is simply too new to be officially adopted, and needed to be challenged, weathered and chewed over to test its robustness before standing a chance of being internationally accepted at UN negotiations."<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2015_update">2015 update</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: 2015 update">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The planetary boundary framework was updated in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> It was suggested that three of the boundaries (including climate change) might push the Earth system into a new state if crossed; these also strongly influence the remaining boundaries. In the paper, the framework is developed to make it more applicable at the regional scale.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_commentary">Other commentary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Planetary_boundaries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Other commentary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.theguardian.com/sustainable-business/planetary-boundaries-limits-earth-podcast">Planetary boundaries: what are the limits of the earth? - podcast</a> <i>The Guardian</i>, 30 January 2013.</li>
